<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Concept">Concept</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Concept">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="11.340045351474" data-mwprovider="local" data-mwtitle="Eliza_musical_motif_in_Hamilton_(musical).ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_2" preload="none" style="width:220px">
<source data-bandwidth="122318" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (122 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b0/Eliza_musical_motif_in_Hamilton_%28musical%29.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/></audio></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eliza_musical_motif_in_Hamilton_(musical).ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The names of many main characters, including that of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Schuyler_Hamilton" title="Elizabeth Schuyler Hamilton">Elizabeth Schuyler Hamilton</a>, are given a unique melodic motif that is repeated throughout the musical <i>Hamilton</i> in various keys, tempos, and timbres depending on the context. This is one example of how <i>Hamilton</i> weaves melodic and lyrical reprises into later songs in the score in order to cue an emotional response.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to an article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>, the show is "an achievement of historical and cultural reimagining." The costumes and set reflect the period, with "velvet frock coats and knee britches. The set ...is a wooden scaffold against exposed brick; the warm lighting suggests candlelight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mead_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-mead-4">[4]</a></sup> The musical is mostly <a href="/wiki/Sung-through" title="Sung-through">sung and rapped all the way through</a>, with little dialogue isolated outside of the musical score.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloom_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-bloom-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donaldson_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Donaldson-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Casting_diversity">Casting diversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Casting diversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Miranda said that the portrayal of Hamilton, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, and other white historical figures by black and Hispanic actors should not require any substantial <a href="/wiki/Suspension_of_disbelief" title="Suspension of disbelief">suspension of disbelief</a> by audience members. "Our cast looks like America looks now, and that's certainly intentional", he said. "It's a way of pulling you into the story and allowing you to leave whatever cultural baggage you have about the founding fathers at the door."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> He noted "We're telling the story of old, dead white men but we're using actors of color, and that makes the story more immediate and more accessible to a contemporary audience."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>The pro-immigration message of Hamilton is at the forefront, as the show revolves around the life of one of the Founding Fathers of America, Alexander Hamilton, and how he made his mark in American politics as an immigrant. Instead of being characterized as a white person, Alexander Hamilton's immigrant status would be referenced throughout the whole show, alongside with the virtue and prowess of Hamilton ("by working a lot harder, by being a lot smarter, by being a self-starter," described in the show's opening), in order to foster a positive image of immigrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Alongside this, the casting of Black, Latino, and Asian American leads allowed audiences to literally view America as a nation of immigrants, with the intention of showing how irrelevant the Founding Fathers' whiteness is to their claim on the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> "<i>Hamilton</i> is a story about America, and the most beautiful thing about it is... it's told by such a diverse cast with a such diverse styles of music," according to Renee Elise Goldberry, who played Angelica Schuyler. "We have the opportunity to reclaim a history that some of us don't necessarily think is our own."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Miranda has stated that he is "totally open" to women playing the Founding Fathers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Casting for the British production is expected to feature predominantly black British artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft.com_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft.com-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_accuracy">Historical accuracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Historical accuracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology_and_events">Chronology and events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Chronology and events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Although <i>Hamilton</i> was based on true events, Miranda did use some dramatic license in retelling the story. For example, while Angelica did have a strong relationship with Hamilton, it was exaggerated in the show. During "Satisfied", Angelica explains why Hamilton is not suitable for her despite wanting him. In particular, she states, "I'm a girl in a world in which my only job is to marry rich. My father has no sons so I'm the one who has to social climb for one." In actuality, Angelica had less pressure on her to do this. <a href="/wiki/Philip_Schuyler" title="Philip Schuyler">Philip Schuyler</a> actually had fifteen children, including two sons who survived into adulthood (one of whom was New York State Assemblyman <a href="/wiki/Philip_Jeremiah_Schuyler" title="Philip Jeremiah Schuyler">Philip Jeremiah Schuyler</a>), and Angelica had eloped with <a href="/wiki/John_Barker_Church" title="John Barker Church">John Barker Church</a> three years before she met Hamilton at her sister's wedding, when she was already mother of two of her eight children with Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Miranda stated that he chose to do this because it is stronger dramatically if Angelica is available but cannot marry him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bustle_accuracy_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-bustle_accuracy-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, in Act I, Burr's role in Hamilton's life is overstated, and much of the early interactions between the two men in the show are fictionalized. For example, while Burr was present at the Battle on Monmouth, Burr did not serve as Charles Lee's second in his duel with John Laurens as seen in "Ten Duel Commandments", Lee's second was Evan Edwards<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Hamilton also never approached Burr to help write the Federalist Papers as portrayed in "Non-Stop".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>During Act I, the character of Aaron Burr says that "...Martha Washington named her feral tomcat after him! [Hamilton]", to which Alexander Hamilton replies: "That's true!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> In fact it is false. The idea of Hamilton as a serial adulterer has been one of the biggest mischaracterizations of the real Alexander Hamilton for centuries, with celebrated authors repeating the story over and over again, notwithstanding that the sexual connotation of <i>tomcat</i> as a womanizer did not appear in dictionaries until the first half of the 20th century. The "tomcat" story has been previously discredited by author Stephen Knott,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> and refuted by historian and author Michael E. Newton at the "Alexander Hamilton Discoveries and Findings" talk held by the Alexander Hamilton Awareness Society at <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Hall" title="Liberty Hall">Liberty Hall</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kean_University" title="Kean University">Kean University</a>) as part of the 2016 CelebrateHAMILTON events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Act II, there are multiple inaccuracies throughout Hamilton's decline, probably due to time constraints and narrative arc. While it is true that John Adams and Hamilton did not particularly get along, John Adams did not fire Hamilton as told in the show. Hamilton tendered his resignation from his position as Secretary of the Treasury on December 1, 1794,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> two years before Adams became president. However, Hamilton remained close friends with Washington and highly influential in the political sphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> In addition, Jefferson, Madison and Burr did not approach Hamilton about his affair, it was actually <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Muhlenberg" title="Frederick Muhlenberg">Frederick Muhlenberg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abraham_B._Venable" title="Abraham B. Venable">Abraham Venable</a> in December 1792.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds_Pamphlet_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds_Pamphlet-121">[121]</a></sup> Monroe was a close friend of Jefferson's and shared the information of Hamilton's affair with him. In the Summer of 1797, journalist <a href="/wiki/James_T._Callender" title="James T. Callender">James Callender</a> broke the story of Hamilton's infidelity. Hamilton blamed Monroe, and the altercation nearly ended in a duel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> With nothing left to do, Hamilton then published the Reynolds pamphlet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> In "Blow Us All Away", <a href="/wiki/George_Eacker" title="George Eacker">George Eacker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Hamilton" title="Philip Hamilton">Philip Hamilton</a> engage in a duel, before the events of the 1800 presidential election. The duel actually occurred in 1801, with Philip Hamilton dying on November 24. In the show, Eacker fires on Philip at the count of seven, while what happened in real-life is almost the opposite; both men refused to fire for over a minute before Eacker shot Philip in the hips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Lastly, it was not the presidential election of 1800 that led to Burr and Hamilton's duel. Burr did become Jefferson's vice-president, but when Jefferson decided to not run with Burr for reelection in 1804, Burr opted to run for <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_York" title="Governor of New York">Governor of New York</a> instead. Burr lost to <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Lewis_(governor)" title="Morgan Lewis (governor)">Morgan Lewis</a> in a landslide. Afterwards, a letter was published from <a href="/wiki/Charles_D._Cooper" title="Charles D. Cooper">Charles D. Cooper</a> to Philip Schuyler, claiming that Hamilton called Burr, "a dangerous man, and one who ought not be trusted with the reins of government", and that he knew of "a still more despicable opinion which General Hamilton has expressed of Mr. Burr."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> This led to the letters between Burr and Hamilton as seen in the show in "Your Obedient Servant."</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_analysis_and_scholarship">Critical analysis and scholarship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Critical analysis and scholarship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The show has been critiqued for a simplistic depiction of Hamilton and vilification of Jefferson. <a href="/wiki/Joanne_B._Freeman" title="Joanne B. Freeman">Joanne B. Freeman</a>, a history professor at <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> contrasted the show's Hamilton to the "real Hamilton [who] was a mass of contradictions: an immigrant who sometimes distrusted immigrants, a revolutionary who placed a supreme value on law and order, a man who distrusted the rumblings of the masses yet preached his politics to them more frequently and passionately than many of his more democracy-friendly fellows."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Australian historian Shane White found the framing of the show's story "troubling," stating that he and many historian colleagues "would like to imagine that <i>Hamilton</i> is a last convulsion of the founding father mythology."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shanewhite_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-shanewhite-128">[128]</a></sup> According to White, Miranda's depiction of the founding of the United States "infuses new life into an older view of American history" that centered on the Founding Fathers, instead of joining the many historians who were "attempting to get away from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Man_theory" title="Great Man theory">Great Men story</a>" by incorporating "ordinary people, African-Americans, Native Americans and women" into a "more inclusive and nuanced" historical narrative in which Hamilton has a "cameo rather than leading role."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shanewhite_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-shanewhite-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rutgers_University" title="Rutgers University">Rutgers University</a> professor Lyra Monteiro criticized the show's multi-ethnic casting as obscuring a complete lack of identifiable enslaved or free persons of color as characters in the show.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-monteiro2016_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-monteiro2016-129">[129]</a></sup> Monteiro identified other commentators, such as Ishmael Reed, who criticized the show for making Hamilton and other historical personages appear more progressive on racial injustice than they really were.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-monteiro2016_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-monteiro2016-129">[129]</a></sup> According to Reed, "[Hamilton's] reputation has been shored up as an abolitionist and someone who was opposed to slavery," which Reed stated was untrue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reed2015_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-reed2015-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Baffler" title="The Baffler">The Baffler</a></i>, policy analyst Matt Stoller criticized the musical's portrayal of Hamilton as an idealist committed to democratic principles, in contrast to what he characterized as the historical record of Hamilton's reactionary, anti-democratic politics and legacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stoller_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-stoller-131">[131]</a></sup> For example, Stoller cited Hamilton as a leader involved in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newburgh_conspiracy" title="Newburgh conspiracy">Newburgh conspiracy</a> (a military coup plot against the Continental Congress in 1783); his development of a national financial system which, in Stoller's view, empowered the plutocratic elite; and his use of military force, indefinite detention, and mass arrests against dissenters during the <a href="/wiki/Whiskey_Rebellion" title="Whiskey Rebellion">Whiskey Rebellion</a> of 1791.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stoller_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-stoller-131">[131]</a></sup> In 2007, history writer William Hogeland criticized Chernow's biography of Hamilton on similar grounds in the <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_Review" title="Boston Review">Boston Review</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_education">Use in education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Use in education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/KQED" title="KQED">KQED News</a> wrote of a "growing number of intrepid U.S. history teachers...who are harnessing the <i>Hamilton</i> phenomenon to inspire their students."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kqed.org_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-kqed.org-133">[133]</a></sup> The Cabinet rap battles provide a way to engage students with topics that have traditionally been considered uninteresting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kqed.org_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-kqed.org-133">[133]</a></sup> An elective course for 11th and 12th graders on the musical <i>Hamilton</i> was held at the <a href="/wiki/Ethical_Culture_Fieldston_School" title="Ethical Culture Fieldston School">Ethical Culture Fieldston School</a> in New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kqed.org_133-2"><a href="#cite_note-kqed.org-133">[133]</a></sup> KQED News added that "<i>Hamilton</i> is especially galvanizing for the student who believes that stories about 18th century America are distant and irrelevant" as it shows the Founding Fathers were real humans with real feeling and real flaws, rather than "bloodless, two-dimensional cutouts who devoted their lives to abstract principles."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kqed.org_133-3"><a href="#cite_note-kqed.org-133">[133]</a></sup> A high school teacher from the Bronx noted his students were "singing these songs the way they might sing the latest release from Drake or Adele."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> One teacher focused on Hamilton's ability to write his way out of trouble and toward a higher plane of existence: "skilled writing is the clearest sign of scholarship—and the best way to rise up and alter your circumstance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Hamilton</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s producers have made a pledge to allow 20,000 <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Department_of_Education" title="New York City Department of Education">New York City public high school</a> students from low-income families to get subsidized tickets to see <i>Hamilton</i> on Broadway by reducing their tickets to $70 for students, and the <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Foundation" title="Rockefeller Foundation">Rockefeller Foundation</a> provided $1.5 million to further lower ticket prices to $10 per student.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNStudents_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-DNStudents-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schussler_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schussler-137">[137]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gilder_Lehrman_Institute_of_American_History" title="Gilder Lehrman Institute of American History">Gilder Lehrman Institute of American History</a> created a study guide to accompany the student-ticket program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNStudents_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-DNStudents-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schussler_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schussler-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>The website <i>EducationWorld</i> writes that <i>Hamilton</i> is "being praised for its revitalization of interest in civic education."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_University" title="Northwestern University">Northwestern University</a> announced plans to offer course work in 2017 inspired by <i>Hamilton</i>, in history, Latino studies, and interdisciplinary studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, Moraine Valley Community College started a Hamilton appreciation movement, Straight Outta Hamilton, hosting panels and events that talk about the musical itself and relate them to current events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_impact">Legacy and impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Legacy and impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="$10_bill"></span><span class="mw-headline" id=".2410_bill">$10 bill</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: $10 bill">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_the_Treasury" title="U.S. Department of the Treasury">U.S. Department of the Treasury</a> announced a redesign to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_ten-dollar_bill" title="United States ten-dollar bill">$10 bill</a>, with plans to replace Hamilton with a then-undecided woman from American history. Because of Hamilton's surging popularity, almost exclusively due to the musical, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Treasury_Secretary" title="United States Treasury Secretary">United States Treasury Secretary</a> <a href="/wiki/Jack_Lew" title="Jack Lew">Jack Lew</a> reversed the plans to replace Hamilton's portrait, instead deciding to replace <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> with <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Tubman" title="Harriet Tubman">Harriet Tubman</a> on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_twenty-dollar_bill" title="United States twenty-dollar bill">$20 bill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hamilton:_The_Revolution"><i>Hamilton: The Revolution</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Hamilton: The Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On April 12, 2016, Miranda and Jeremy McCarter's book, <i>Hamilton: The Revolution</i>, was released, detailing <i>Hamilton</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s journey from an idea to a successful Broadway musical. It includes an inside look at not only Alexander Hamilton's revolution, but the cultural revolution that permeates the show. It also has footnotes from Miranda and stories from behind the scenes of the show.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Hamilton's_America"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hamilton.27s_America"><i>Hamilton's America</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Hamilton's America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After premiering on the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Film_Festival" title="New York Film Festival">New York Film Festival</a> on October 1, 2016, <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Performances" title="Great Performances">Great Performances</a></i> exhibited on October 21, 2016 the documentary <i>Hamilton's America</i>. Directed by Alex Horwitz, it "delves even deeper into the creation of the show, revealing Miranda's process of absorbing and then adapting Hamilton's epic story into groundbreaking musical theater. Further fleshing out the story is newly shot footage of the New York production with its original cast, trips to historic locations such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mt._Vernon" title="Mt. Vernon">Mt. Vernon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valley_Forge" title="Valley Forge">Valley Forge</a> with Miranda and other cast members, and a surprising range of interviews with prominent personalities, experts, politicians, and musicians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> The film featured interviews with American historians and Hamilton authorities <a href="/wiki/Ron_Chernow" title="Ron Chernow">Ron Chernow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joanne_B._Freeman" title="Joanne B. Freeman">Joanne B. Freeman</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="2016_Vice_President–elect_Pence_controversy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2016_Vice_President.E2.80.93elect_Pence_controversy">2016 Vice President–elect Pence controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: 2016 Vice President–elect Pence controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following a performance on November 18, 2016, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vice_President-elect_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President-elect of the United States">Vice President-elect</a> <a href="/wiki/Mike_Pence" title="Mike Pence">Mike Pence</a> in the audience, <a href="/wiki/Brandon_Victor_Dixon" title="Brandon Victor Dixon">Brandon Victor Dixon</a> addressed Pence from the stage with a statement jointly written by the cast, show creator Lin-Manuel Miranda and producer Jeffrey Seller.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DixonStatement_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-DixonStatement-146">[146]</a></sup> Dixon began by quieting the audience, and stated:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Vice President-elect Pence, we welcome you and we truly thank you for joining us here at <i>Hamilton: An American Musical</i>, we really do. We, sir,—we—are the diverse America who are alarmed and anxious that your new administration will not protect us, our planet, our children, our parents, or defend us and uphold our inalienable rights, sir. But we truly hope that this show has inspired you to uphold our American values and to work on behalf of all of us. All of us. Again, we truly thank you truly for seeing this show, this wonderful American story told by a diverse group of men and women of different colors, creeds and orientations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DixonStatement_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-DixonStatement-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Pence listened to the expression of concern about <a href="/wiki/President-elect_of_the_United_States" title="President-elect of the United States">President-elect</a> <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Donald_Trump" title="Presidency of Donald Trump">upcoming administration</a> and later expressed that he was not offended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> However, Trump demanded an apology<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> for what he described, on Twitter, as the cast having "harassed" Pence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> This led to an online campaign called "#BoycottHamilton," which became widely mocked as the show is already sold-out months in advance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Trump was criticized by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i>, who noted the division between white and non-white America in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="United States presidential election, 2016">2016 Presidential election</a> and suggested Trump could have offered "assurances that he would be a president for all Americans—that he would respect everybody regardless of race or gender or creed";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WashPostCriticism_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-WashPostCriticism-151">[151]</a></sup> instead, as Presidential historian <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dallek" title="Robert Dallek">Robert Dallek</a> expressed, Trump's Twitter response was a "striking act of divisiveness by an incoming president struggling to heal the nation after a bitter election",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WashPostCriticism_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-WashPostCriticism-151">[151]</a></sup> with the <i>Hamilton</i> cast a proxy for those fearful of Trump's policies and rhetoric. Jeffrey Seller, the show's lead producer, said that while Trump has not seen <i>Hamilton</i> or inquired about tickets, he is "welcome to attend".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parodies">Parodies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Parodies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In April 2016, <i>Jeb! The Musical</i>, subtitled <i>An American Disappointment</i> appeared on the Internet with <a href="/wiki/Jeb_Bush" title="Jeb Bush">Jeb Bush</a> in the place of Alexander Hamilton,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> with political figures like <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chris_Christie" title="Chris Christie">Chris Christie</a> holding supporting roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> A staged reading, given "just as much preparation as Jeb's campaign," was staged at <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_University" title="Northwestern University">Northwestern University</a> in June of that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> The parody was crowdsourced, with contributions coming from a range of writers. A number of writers were drawn from <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boston_University" title="Boston University">Boston University</a>, <a href="/wiki/McGill_University" title="McGill University">McGill University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a>. These writers met in a Facebook group named "Post Aesthetics".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Alessandrini" title="Gerard Alessandrini">Gerard Alessandrini</a>, the creator of <i><a href="/wiki/Forbidden_Broadway" title="Forbidden Broadway">Forbidden Broadway</a></i>, wrote the revue <i><a href="/wiki/Spamilton" title="Spamilton">Spamilton</a></i> which premiered at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triad_Theater" title="Triad Theater">Triad Theater</a> in New York and also played at the Royal George Theatre in Chicago. It parodies <i>Hamilton</i> and other Broadway shows and caricatures various Broadway stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 12, 2016, the American sitcom <i><a href="/wiki/Modern_Family" title="Modern Family">Modern Family</a></i> released the episode "<a href="/wiki/Weathering_Heights" title="Weathering Heights">Weathering Heights</a>". The episode features a scene where Manny applies for college. To do so he records a parody of "<a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton_(song)" title="Alexander Hamilton (song)">Alexander Hamilton</a>" as part of his application, complete with rewritten lyrics to accompany to his own life. It is revealed that most of the other applications are also <i>Hamilton</i> parodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hamilton_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-atlantic-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-atlantic_5-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Kornhaber, Spencer (March 2016). <a class="external text" href="https://www.theatlantic.com/entertainment/archive/2016/03/hamilton-casting/476247/" rel="nofollow">"<i>Hamilton</i>: Casting After Colorblindness"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">The Atlantic</a></i>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170607115823/https://www.theatlantic.com/entertainment/archive/2016/03/hamilton-casting/476247/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on June 7, 2017. <q><i>Hamilton</i> is not, by the common definition, colorblind. It does not merely allow for some of the Founding Fathers to be played by people of color.
